<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Ethanol</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For ethanol as a drug or medicine, see <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(drug)" title="Alcohol (drug)">Alcohol (drug)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(medicine)" title="Alcohol (medicine)">Alcohol (medicine)</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethenol" title="Ethenol">ethenol</a>, also known as vinyl alcohol, or <a href="/wiki/Ethynol" title="Ethynol">ethynol</a>.</div>

<tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;">
</td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Ethanol</b>, also called <b>alcohol</b>, <b>ethyl alcohol</b>, <b>grain alcohol</b>, and <b>drinking alcohol</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a>, a simple <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>OH</span>. Its formula can be also written as <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>−<span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>−<span class="chemf nowrap">OH</span> or <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>−<span class="chemf nowrap">OH</span> (an <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_group" title="Ethyl group">ethyl group</a> linked to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> group), and is often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbreviated" title="Abbreviated">abbreviated</a> as <b>EtOH</b>. Ethanol is a <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flammability" title="Flammability">flammable</a>, colorless liquid with a slight characteristic odor. It is a <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(drug)" title="Alcohol (drug)">psychoactive substance</a> and is the principal type of alcohol found in <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_drink" title="Alcoholic drink">alcoholic drinks</a>.
</p><p>Ethanol is naturally produced by the <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">fermentation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a> by <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeasts</a> or via <a href="/wiki/Petrochemistry" title="Petrochemistry">petrochemical</a> processes, and is most commonly consumed as a popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recreational_drug" title="Recreational drug">recreational drug</a>. It also has medical applications as an <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Disinfectant" title="Disinfectant">disinfectant</a>. The compound is widely used as a <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemical</a> <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a>, either for scientific chemical testing or in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">synthesis</a> of other <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>, and is a vital substance used across many different kinds of manufacturing industries. Ethanol is also used as a clean-burning <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_fuel" title="Alcohol fuel">fuel</a> source.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Ethanol</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Systematic_name" title="Systematic name">systematic name</a> defined by the <a href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature_of_organic_chemistry" title="IUPAC nomenclature of organic chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry (IUPAC)</a> for a compound consisting of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkyl_group" title="Alkyl group">alkyl group</a> with two carbon <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> (prefix “eth-”), having a single bond between them (infix “-an-”), attached <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional group</a> −OH group (suffix “-ol”).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pubchem_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pubchem-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The “eth-” prefix and the qualifier “ethyl” in “ethyl alcohol” originally come from the name “ethyl” assigned in 1834 to the group <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>− by <a href="/wiki/Justus_von_Liebig" title="Justus von Liebig">Justus Liebig</a>. He coined the word from the <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> name <i>Aether</i> of the compound <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>−O−<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span> (commonly called “ether” in <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, more specifically called “<a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">diethyl ether</a>”).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a>, <i>Ethyl</i> is a contraction of the Ancient Greek <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B1%E1%BC%B0%CE%B8%CE%AE%CF%81#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:αἰθήρ">αἰθήρ</a> (<i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language transliteration">aithḗr</i></i>, “upper air”) and the Greek word <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BD%95%CE%BB%CE%B7#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:ὕλη">ὕλη</a> (<i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language transliteration">hýlē</i></i>, “substance”).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The name <i>ethanol</i> was coined as a result of a resolution that was adopted at the International Conference on Chemical Nomenclature that was held in April 1892 in Geneva, Switzerland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term “<a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a>” now refers to a wider class of substances in chemistry nomenclature, but in common parlance it remains the name of ethanol. The Oxford English Dictionary claims that it is a medieval loan from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Kohl_(cosmetics)" title="Kohl (cosmetics)">al-kuḥl</a></i>, a powdered ore of antimony used since antiquity as a cosmetic, and retained that meaning in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Latin" title="Middle Latin">Middle Latin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The use of “alcohol” for ethanol (in full, “alcohol of wine”) is modern, first recorded 1753, and by the later 17th century referred to “any sublimated substance; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distilled_beverage" title="Distilled beverage">distilled spirit</a>” use for “the spirit of wine” (shortened from a full expression <i>alcohol of wine</i>). The systematic use in chemistry dates to 1850.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical">Medical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(medicine)" title="Alcohol (medicine)">Alcohol (medicine)</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiseptic">Antiseptic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Antiseptic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ethanol is used in medical wipes and most commonly in antibacterial <a href="/wiki/Hand_sanitizer" title="Hand sanitizer">hand sanitizer</a> gels as an <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic</a>. Ethanol kills organisms by <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(biochemistry)" title="Denaturation (biochemistry)">denaturing</a> their <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and dissolving their <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a> and is effective against most <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>, and many <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>. However, ethanol is ineffective against bacterial <a href="/wiki/Endospore" title="Endospore">spores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> 70% ethanol is the most effective concentration, particularly because of osmotic pressure. Absolute ethanol may inactivate microbes without destroying them because the alcohol is unable to fully permeate the microbe's membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Antidote">Antidote</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Antidote">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ethanol may be administered as an <a href="/wiki/Antidote" title="Antidote">antidote</a> to <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_glycol" title="Ethylene glycol">ethylene glycol</a> poisoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicinal_solvent">Medicinal solvent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Medicinal solvent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ethanol, often in high concentrations, is used to dissolve many water-insoluble medications and related compounds. Liquid preparations of crack cocaine, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analgesics" title="Analgesics">pain medication</a>, and mouth washes may be dissolved in 1 to 25% concentrations of ethanol and may need to be avoided in individuals with adverse reactions to ethanol such as <a href="/wiki/Alcohol-induced_respiratory_reactions" title="Alcohol-induced respiratory reactions">alcohol-induced respiratory reactions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ann_Allergy_Asthma_Immunol_2013_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ann_Allergy_Asthma_Immunol_2013-19">[19]</a></sup> Ethanol is present mainly as an antimicrobial preservative in over 700 liquid preparations of medicine including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetaminophen" title="Acetaminophen">acetaminophen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron_supplement" title="Iron supplement">iron supplements</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ranitidine" title="Ranitidine">ranitidine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Furosemide" title="Furosemide">furosemide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mannitol" title="Mannitol">mannitol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phenobarbital" title="Phenobarbital">phenobarbital</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole" title="Trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole">trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Over-the-counter" title="Over-the-counter">over-the-counter</a> <a href="/wiki/Cough_medicine" title="Cough medicine">cough medicine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacology">Pharmacology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pharmacology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ethyl Alcohol is extensively metabolized by the liver, particularly via the enzyme  CYP450.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harger_1958_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harger_1958-21">[21]</a></sup> Ethyl Alcohol increases the secretion of acids in the stomach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harger_1958_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harger_1958-21">[21]</a></sup> The metabolite acetaldehyde is responsible for much of the short term, and long term effects of ethyl alcohol toxicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallner_2008_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wallner_2008-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreational">Recreational</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Recreational">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(drug)" title="Alcohol (drug)">Alcohol (drug)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_drink" title="Alcoholic drink">Alcoholic drink</a></div>
<p>As a <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> <a href="/wiki/Depressant" title="Depressant">depressant</a>, ethanol is one of the most commonly consumed <a href="/wiki/Psychoactive_drug" title="Psychoactive drug">psychoactive drugs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel">Fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Engine_fuel">Engine fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Engine fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">Ethanol fuel</a></div>

<p>The largest single use of ethanol is as an engine <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_additive" title="Fuel additive">fuel additive</a>. <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> in particular relies heavily upon the use of ethanol as an engine fuel, due in part to its role as the globe's leading producer of ethanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">Gasoline</a> sold in Brazil contains at least 25% <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous" title="Anhydrous">anhydrous</a> ethanol. Hydrous ethanol (about 95% ethanol and 5% water) can be used as fuel in more than 90% of new gasoline fueled cars sold in the country. Brazilian ethanol is produced from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a> and noted for high <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">carbon sequestration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPo-Brazil_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-WaPo-Brazil-29">[29]</a></sup> The US and many other countries primarily use E10 (10% ethanol, sometimes known as gasohol) and E85 (85% ethanol) ethanol/gasoline mixtures.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethyl_alcohol_usp_grade.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1892" data-file-width="1036" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Ethyl_alcohol_usp_grade.jpg/170px-Ethyl_alcohol_usp_grade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Ethyl_alcohol_usp_grade.jpg/255px-Ethyl_alcohol_usp_grade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Ethyl_alcohol_usp_grade.jpg/340px-Ethyl_alcohol_usp_grade.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethyl_alcohol_usp_grade.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/United_States_Pharmacopeia" title="United States Pharmacopeia">USP</a> grade ethanol for laboratory use.</div></div></div>
<p>Ethanol has been used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_fuel" title="Rocket fuel">rocket fuel</a> and is currently in <a href="/wiki/Light_aircraft" title="Light aircraft">lightweight</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mark-III_X-racer" title="Mark-III X-racer">rocket-powered racing aircraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdc20100426_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-sdc20100426-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Australian law limits the use of pure ethanol from sugarcane waste to 10% in automobiles. Older cars (and vintage cars designed to use a slower burning fuel) should have the engine valves upgraded or replaced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to an industry <a href="/wiki/Advocacy_group" title="Advocacy group">advocacy group</a>, ethanol as a fuel reduces harmful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle_emissions" title="Motor vehicle emissions">tailpipe emissions</a> of carbon monoxide, particulate matter, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxides_of_nitrogen" title="Oxides of nitrogen">oxides of nitrogen</a>, and other ozone-forming pollutants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory</a> analyzed greenhouse gas emissions of many different engine and fuel combinations, and found that <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a>/petrodiesel blend (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B20_(biodiesel)" title="B20 (biodiesel)">B20</a>) showed a reduction of 8%, conventional <a href="/wiki/E85" title="E85">E85</a> ethanol blend a reduction of 17% and <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">cellulosic ethanol</a> 64%, compared with pure gasoline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ethanol combustion in an internal combustion engine yields many of the products of incomplete combustion produced by gasoline and significantly larger amounts of <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> and related species such as acetaldehyde.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> This leads to a significantly larger photochemical reactivity and more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_level_ozone" title="Ground level ozone">ground level ozone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> These data have been assembled into The Clean Fuels Report comparison of fuel emissions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and show that ethanol exhaust generates 2.14 times as much ozone as gasoline exhaust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> When this is added into the custom <i>Localised Pollution Index (LPI)</i> of The Clean Fuels Report, the local pollution of ethanol (pollution that contributes to smog) is rated 1.7, where gasoline is 1.0 and higher numbers signify greater pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/California_Air_Resources_Board" title="California Air Resources Board">California Air Resources Board</a> formalized this issue in 2008 by recognizing control standards for formaldehydes as an emissions control group, much like the conventional <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NOx</a> and Reactive Organic Gases (ROGs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>World production of ethanol in 2006 was 51 gigalitres (1.3<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> US gal), with 69% of the world supply coming from Brazil and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> More than 20% of Brazilian cars are able to use 100% ethanol as fuel, which includes ethanol-only engines and <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flex-fuel</a> engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Flex-fuel engines in Brazil are able to work with all ethanol, all gasoline or any mixture of both. In the US flex-fuel vehicles can run on 0% to 85% ethanol (15% gasoline) since higher ethanol blends are not yet allowed or efficient. Brazil supports this population of ethanol-burning automobiles with large national infrastructure that produces ethanol from domestically grown <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">Sugar cane</a> not only has a greater concentration of sucrose than corn (by about 30%), but is also much easier to extract. The <a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">bagasse</a> generated by the process is not wasted, but is used in power plants to produce electricity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the ethanol fuel industry is based largely on <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>. According to the Renewable Fuels Association, as of 30 October 2007, 131 grain ethanol bio-refineries in the United States have the capacity to produce 7.0 billion US gallons (26,000,000 m<sup>3</sup>) of ethanol per year. An additional 72 construction projects underway (in the U.S.) can add 6.4 billion US gallons (24,000,000 m<sup>3</sup>) of new capacity in the next 18 months. Over time, it is believed that a material portion of the ≈150-billion-US-gallon (570,000,000 m<sup>3</sup>) per year market for gasoline will begin to be replaced with fuel ethanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rfa1_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-rfa1-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sweet_sorghum" title="Sweet sorghum">Sweet sorghum</a> is another potential source of ethanol, and is suitable for growing in dryland conditions. The <a href="/wiki/International_Crops_Research_Institute_for_the_Semi-Arid_Tropics" title="International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics">International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICRISAT" title="ICRISAT">ICRISAT</a>) is investigating the possibility of growing sorghum as a source of fuel, food, and animal feed in arid parts of <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sweet_sorghum" title="Sweet sorghum">Sweet sorghum</a> has one-third the water requirement of sugarcane over the same time period. It also requires about 22% less water than corn (also known as maize). The world’s first sweet sorghum ethanol distillery began commercial production in 2007 in Andhra Pradesh, India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ethanol's high <a href="/wiki/Miscibility" title="Miscibility">miscibility</a> with water makes it unsuitable for shipping through modern <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a> like liquid hydrocarbons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HornKrupp2009_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-HornKrupp2009-45">[45]</a></sup> Mechanics have seen increased cases of damage to small engines (in particular, the <a href="/wiki/Carburetor" title="Carburetor">carburetor</a>) and attribute the damage to the increased water retention by ethanol in fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rocket_fuel">Rocket fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Rocket fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ethanol was commonly used as fuel in early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipropellant" title="Bipropellant">bipropellant</a> <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a> (liquid propelled) vehicles, in conjunction with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidizer" title="Oxidizer">oxidizer</a> such as liquid oxygen. The German <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">V-2 rocket</a> of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, credited with beginning the space age, used ethanol as the main constituent of <a href="/wiki/List_of_stoffs#List" title="List of stoffs"><i>B-Stoff</i></a>, under such nomenclature the ethanol was mixed with 25% of water to reduce the combustion chamber temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-braeunig_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-braeunig-48">[48]</a></sup> The V-2's design team helped develop U.S. rockets following World War II, including the ethanol-fueled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Redstone_(rocket)" title="Redstone (rocket)">Redstone rocket</a> which launched the first U.S. satellite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Alcohols fell into general disuse as more efficient rocket fuels were developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-braeunig_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-braeunig-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_cells">Fuel cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fuel cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Commercial fuel cells operate on reformed natural gas, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>. Ethanol is an attractive alternative due to its wide availability, low cost, high purity and low toxicity. There are a wide range of fuel cell concepts that have been trialled including <a href="/wiki/Direct-ethanol_fuel_cell" title="Direct-ethanol fuel cell">direct-ethanol fuel cells</a>, auto-thermal reforming systems and thermally integrated systems. The majority of work is being conducted at a research level although there are a number of organizations at the beginning of commercialization of ethanol fuel cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Household_heating">Household heating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Household heating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ethanol fireplaces can be used for home heating or for decoration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feedstock">Feedstock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Feedstock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="#Reactions"> § Reactions</a></div>
<p>Ethanol is an important industrial ingredient. It has widespread use as a precursor for other organic compounds such as ethyl <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halides</a>, ethyl <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a>, diethyl ether, acetic acid, and ethyl <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solvent">Solvent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Solvent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethanol is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miscible" title="Miscible">miscible</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_(molecule)" title="Water (molecule)">water</a> and is a good general purpose <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a>. It is found in <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">paints</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tincture" title="Tincture">tinctures</a>, markers, and personal care products such as mouthwashes, perfumes and deodorants. However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polysaccharides" title="Polysaccharides">polysaccharides</a> <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_precipitation" title="Ethanol precipitation">precipitate</a> from aqueous solution in the presence of alcohol, and ethanol precipitation is used for this reason in the purification of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-temperature_liquid">Low-temperature liquid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Low-temperature liquid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because of its low melting point (−114.14 °C) and low toxicity, ethanol is sometimes used in laboratories (with <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a> or other coolants) as a <a href="/wiki/Cooling_bath" title="Cooling bath">cooling bath</a> to keep vessels at temperatures below the freezing point of water. For the same reason, it is also used as the active fluid in <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_thermometer" title="Alcohol thermometer">alcohol thermometers</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_(data_page)" title="Ethanol (data page)">Ethanol (data page)</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_formula">Chemical formula</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Chemical formula">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethanol is a 2-carbon alcohol. Its <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">molecular formula</a> is CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH. An alternative notation is CH<sub>3</sub>−CH<sub>2</sub>−OH, which indicates that the carbon of a <a href="/wiki/Methyl_group" title="Methyl group">methyl group</a> (CH<sub>3</sub>−) is attached to the carbon of a <a href="/wiki/Methylene_group" title="Methylene group">methylene group</a> (−CH<sub>2</sub>–), which is attached to the oxygen of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl group (−OH)</a>. It is a constitutional <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomer</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_ether" title="Dimethyl ether">dimethyl ether</a>. Ethanol is sometimes abbreviated as <b>EtOH</b>, using the common organic chemistry notation of representing the ethyl group (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>−) with <b>Et</b>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spiritusflamme_mit_spektrum.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="214" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Spiritusflamme_mit_spektrum.png/170px-Spiritusflamme_mit_spektrum.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5d/Spiritusflamme_mit_spektrum.png 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spiritusflamme_mit_spektrum.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ethanol burning with its spectrum depicted</div></div></div>
<p>Ethanol is a volatile, colorless liquid that has a slight odor. It burns with a smokeless blue flame that is not always visible in normal light. The physical properties of ethanol stem primarily from the presence of its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> group and the shortness of its carbon chain. Ethanol's hydroxyl group is able to participate in hydrogen bonding, rendering it more viscous and less volatile than less polar organic compounds of similar molecular weight, such as <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>.
</p><p>Ethanol is slightly more refractive than water, having a <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> of 1.36242 (at λ=589.3 nm and 18.35 °C or 65.03 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crc_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-crc-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> for ethanol is <span class="nowrap">150 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a></span> at a pressure of <span class="nowrap">4.3 × 10<sup>−4</sup> <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">Pa</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solvent_properties">Solvent properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Solvent properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethanol is a versatile solvent, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miscible" title="Miscible">miscible</a> with water and with many organic solvents, including <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrachloride" title="Carbon tetrachloride">carbon tetrachloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloroform" title="Chloroform">chloroform</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">diethyl ether</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_glycol" title="Ethylene glycol">ethylene glycol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitromethane" title="Nitromethane">nitromethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyridine" title="Pyridine">pyridine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Toluene" title="Toluene">toluene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crc_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-crc-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merck_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-merck-54">[54]</a></sup> It is also miscible with light aliphatic hydrocarbons, such as <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a>, and with aliphatic chlorides such as <a href="/wiki/1,1,1-Trichloroethane" title="1,1,1-Trichloroethane">trichloroethane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tetrachloroethylene" title="Tetrachloroethylene">tetrachloroethylene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merck_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-merck-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ethanol's miscibility with water contrasts with the immiscibility of longer-chain alcohols (five or more carbon atoms), whose water miscibility decreases sharply as the number of carbons increases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m_and_b_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-m_and_b-55">[55]</a></sup> The miscibility of ethanol with <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a> is limited to alkanes up to <a href="/wiki/Undecane" title="Undecane">undecane</a>: mixtures with <a href="/wiki/Dodecane" title="Dodecane">dodecane</a> and higher alkanes show a <a href="/wiki/Miscibility_gap" title="Miscibility gap">miscibility gap</a> below a certain temperature (about 13 °C for dodecane<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>). The miscibility gap tends to get wider with higher alkanes and the temperature for complete miscibility increases.
</p><p>Ethanol-water mixtures have less volume than the sum of their individual components at the given fractions. Mixing equal volumes of ethanol and water results in only 1.92 volumes of mixture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crc_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-crc-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChemTech_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChemTech-57">[57]</a></sup> Mixing ethanol and water is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a>, with up to 777 J/mol<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> being released at 298 K.
</p><p>Mixtures of ethanol and water form an <a href="/wiki/Azeotrope" title="Azeotrope">azeotrope</a> at about 89 mole-% ethanol and 11 mole-% water<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> or a mixture of 95.6 percent ethanol by mass (or about 97% <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_by_volume" title="Alcohol by volume">alcohol by volume</a>) at normal pressure, which boils at 351K (78 °C). This azeotropic composition is strongly temperature- and pressure-dependent and vanishes at temperatures below 303 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="814" data-file-width="1100" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png/300px-Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png/450px-Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png/600px-Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hydrogen bonding in solid ethanol at −186 °C</div></div></div>
<p>Hydrogen bonding causes pure ethanol to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a> to the extent that it readily absorbs water from the air. The polar nature of the hydroxyl group causes ethanol to dissolve many ionic compounds, notably <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_chloride" title="Magnesium chloride">magnesium chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium_chloride" title="Calcium chloride">calcium chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_chloride" title="Ammonium chloride">ammonium chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_bromide" title="Ammonium bromide">ammonium bromide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_bromide" title="Sodium bromide">sodium bromide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merck_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-merck-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">Sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chlorides</a> are slightly soluble in ethanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merck_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-merck-54">[54]</a></sup> Because the ethanol molecule also has a nonpolar end, it will also dissolve nonpolar substances, including most <a href="/wiki/Essential_oil" title="Essential oil">essential oils</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merckoils_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-merckoils-61">[61]</a></sup> and numerous flavoring, coloring, and medicinal agents.
</p><p>The addition of even a few percent of ethanol to water sharply reduces the <a href="/wiki/Surface_tension" title="Surface tension">surface tension</a> of water. This property partially explains the "<a href="/wiki/Tears_of_wine" title="Tears of wine">tears of wine</a>" phenomenon. When wine is swirled in a glass, ethanol evaporates quickly from the thin film of wine on the wall of the glass. As the wine's ethanol content decreases, its surface tension increases and the thin film "beads up" and runs down the glass in channels rather than as a smooth sheet.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flammability">Flammability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Flammability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An ethanol-water solution that contains 40% alcohol by weight (about 56% by volume) will catch fire if heated to about 26 °C (79 °F) and if an ignition source is applied to it. This is called its <a href="/wiki/Flash_point" title="Flash point">flash point</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flash_point_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-flash_point-62">[62]</a></sup> The flash point of pure ethanol is 16.60 °C (61.88 °F), less than average room temperature. 
</p>
