<p>Dishes using burning alcohol for culinary effects are called <a href="/wiki/Flamb%C3%A9" title="Flambé">Flambé</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_occurrence">Natural occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Natural occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Ethanol is a byproduct of the <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolic process</a> of yeast. As such, ethanol will be present in any yeast habitat. Ethanol can commonly be found in overripe fruit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Ethanol produced by symbiotic yeast can be found in <a href="/wiki/Palm_wine" title="Palm wine">bertam palm</a> blossoms. Although some animal species such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentailed_treeshrew" title="Pentailed treeshrew">pentailed treeshrew</a> exhibit ethanol-seeking behaviors, most show no interest or avoidance of food sources containing ethanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Ethanol is also produced during the germination of many plants as a result of natural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anerobiosis" title="Anerobiosis">anerobiosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Ethanol has been detected in <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>, forming an icy coating around dust grains in <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_cloud" title="Interstellar cloud">interstellar clouds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
Minute quantity amounts (average 196 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">ppb</a>) of endogenous ethanol and acetaldehyde were found in the exhaled breath of healthy volunteers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Auto-brewery_syndrome" title="Auto-brewery syndrome">Auto-brewery syndrome</a>, also known as gut fermentation syndrome, is a rare medical condition in which <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_intoxication" title="Alcohol intoxication">intoxicating</a> quantities of ethanol are produced through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endogenous" title="Endogenous">endogenous</a> <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">fermentation</a> within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_system" title="Digestive system">digestive system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethanol_Flasche.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="609" data-file-width="382" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Ethanol_Flasche.jpg/170px-Ethanol_Flasche.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Ethanol_Flasche.jpg/255px-Ethanol_Flasche.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Ethanol_Flasche.jpg/340px-Ethanol_Flasche.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethanol_Flasche.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>94% denatured ethanol sold in a bottle for household use</div></div></div>
<p>Ethanol is produced both as a <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemical</a>, through the hydration of ethylene and, via biological processes, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermenting</a> sugars with <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills-Ecklund_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mills-Ecklund-70">[70]</a></sup> Which process is more economical depends on prevailing prices of petroleum and grain feed stocks. In the 1970s most industrial ethanol in the United States was made as a petrochemical, but in the 1980s the United States introduced subsidies for corn-based ethanol and today it is almost all made from that source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WittcoffReuben2004_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-WittcoffReuben2004-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethylene_hydration">Ethylene hydration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Ethylene hydration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethanol for use as an industrial feedstock or solvent (sometimes referred to as synthetic ethanol) is made from <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemical</a> feed stocks, primarily by the <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a>-<a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyzed</a> hydration of ethylene:
</p>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene"><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>OH</span></dd></dl>
<p>The catalyst is most commonly <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">phosphoric acid</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r_and_c_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-r_and_c-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ullmann_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorbed</a> onto a porous support such as <a href="/wiki/Silica_gel" title="Silica gel">silica gel</a> or <a href="/wiki/Diatomaceous_earth" title="Diatomaceous earth">diatomaceous earth</a>. This catalyst was first used for large-scale ethanol production by the <a href="/wiki/Shell_Oil_Company" title="Shell Oil Company">Shell Oil Company</a> in 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECT4_820_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECT4_820-74">[74]</a></sup> The reaction is carried out in the presence of high pressure steam at 300 °C (572 °F) where a 5:3 ethylene to steam ratio is maintained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In the U.S., this process was used on an industrial scale by <a href="/wiki/Union_Carbide" title="Union Carbide">Union Carbide Corporation</a> and others, but now only <a href="/wiki/LyondellBasell" title="LyondellBasell">LyondellBasell</a> uses it commercially.
</p><p>In an older process, first practiced on the industrial scale in 1930 by Union Carbide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECT4_817_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECT4_817-77">[77]</a></sup> but now almost entirely obsolete, ethylene was hydrated indirectly by reacting it with concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_sulfate" title="Ethyl sulfate">ethyl sulfate</a>, which was <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolyzed</a> to yield ethanol and regenerate the sulfuric acid:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s_and_h_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-s_and_h-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a> → <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_sulfate" title="Ethyl sulfate"><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>H</a></dd>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Ethyl_sulfate" title="Ethyl sulfate"><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>C</span><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>H</a> + <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H2O" title="H2O"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></a> → <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>C</span><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>H + <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_CO2">From CO<sub>2</sub></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: From CO2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>CO<sub>2</sub> can also be used as the <a href="/wiki/Raw_material" title="Raw material">raw material</a>.
</p><p>CO<sub>2</sub> can be reduced by hydrogen to produce ethanol, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, and smaller amounts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2,3-butanediol" title="2,3-butanediol">2,3-butanediol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a> using <a href="/wiki/Clostridium_ljungdahlii" title="Clostridium ljungdahlii">Clostridium ljungdahlii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clostridium_autoethanogenum" title="Clostridium autoethanogenum">Clostridium autoethanogenum</a> or <a href="/wiki/Moorella_(bacterium)" title="Moorella (bacterium)">Moorella sp. HUC22-1</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>CO<sub>2</sub> can be converted using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrochemical" title="Electrochemical">electrochemical</a> reactions at room temperature and pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> In a system developed at <a href="/wiki/Delft_University_of_Technology" title="Delft University of Technology">Delft University of Technology</a>, a copper <a href="/wiki/Nanowire" title="Nanowire">nanowire</a> array used as a cathode adsorbs molecules of carbon dioxide and reduced intermediate species such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">CO</a> and COH. However, even in the best results about half the current went into producing hydrogen and only a small amount of ethanol was produced. Other products, produced in larger quantities, were (in decreasing order) <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">formic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a>, CO, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-propanol" title="N-propanol">n-propanol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_lipids">From lipids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: From lipids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lipids can also be used to make ethanol and can be found in such raw materials such as <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermentation">Fermentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Fermentation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">Ethanol fermentation</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Yeast_in_winemaking" title="Yeast in winemaking">Yeast in winemaking</a></div>
<p>Ethanol in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcoholic beverages</a> and fuel is produced by fermentation. Certain species of <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a> (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</a></i>) <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolize</a> <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">sugar</a>, producing ethanol and carbon dioxide. The chemical equations below summarize the conversion:
</p>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose"><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span></a> → 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>H + 2 CO<sub>2</sub></dd>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose"><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">22</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">11</sub></span></span></a> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → 4 <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>H + 4 CO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Fermentation is the process of culturing yeast under favorable thermal conditions to produce alcohol. This process is carried out at around 35–40 °C (95–104 °F). Toxicity of ethanol to yeast limits the ethanol concentration obtainable by brewing; higher concentrations, therefore, are obtained by <a href="/wiki/Fortified_wine" title="Fortified wine">fortification</a> or <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a>. The most ethanol-tolerant yeast strains can survive up to approximately 18% ethanol by volume.
</p><p>To produce ethanol from starchy materials such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cereal_grain" title="Cereal grain">cereal grains</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> must first be converted into sugars. In brewing <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>, this has traditionally been accomplished by allowing the grain to germinate, or <a href="/wiki/Malt" title="Malt">malt</a>, which produces the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> <a href="/wiki/Amylase" title="Amylase">amylase</a>. When the malted grain is <a href="/wiki/Mashing" title="Mashing">mashed</a>, the amylase converts the remaining starches into sugars.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cellulose">Cellulose</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Cellulose">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">Cellulosic ethanol</a></div>
<p>Sugars for <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">ethanol fermentation</a> can be obtained from <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>. Deployment of this technology could turn a number of cellulose-containing agricultural by-products, such as <a href="/wiki/Corncob" title="Corncob">corncobs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Straw" title="Straw">straw</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sawdust" title="Sawdust">sawdust</a>, into renewable energy resources. Other agricultural residues such as sugar cane bagasse and <a href="/wiki/Energy_crop" title="Energy crop">energy crops</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Switchgrass" title="Switchgrass">switchgrass</a> may also be a sources of fermentable sugars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Testing">Testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EthanolMIRInfraredSpectra.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="607" data-file-width="985" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/EthanolMIRInfraredSpectra.PNG/300px-EthanolMIRInfraredSpectra.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/EthanolMIRInfraredSpectra.PNG/450px-EthanolMIRInfraredSpectra.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/EthanolMIRInfraredSpectra.PNG/600px-EthanolMIRInfraredSpectra.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EthanolMIRInfraredSpectra.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Infrared reflection spectra of liquid ethanol, showing the -OH band centered at ≈3300 cm<sup>−1</sup> and C-H bands at ≈2950 cm<sup>−1</sup>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethanol_near_IR_spectrum.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2442" data-file-width="3500" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ethanol_near_IR_spectrum.png/300px-Ethanol_near_IR_spectrum.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ethanol_near_IR_spectrum.png/450px-Ethanol_near_IR_spectrum.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ethanol_near_IR_spectrum.png/600px-Ethanol_near_IR_spectrum.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethanol_near_IR_spectrum.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_infrared_spectrum" title="Near infrared spectrum">Near infrared spectrum</a> of liquid ethanol.</div></div></div>
<p>Breweries and <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> plants employ two methods for measuring ethanol concentration. Infrared ethanol sensors measure the vibrational frequency of dissolved ethanol using the CH band at 2900 cm<sup>−1</sup>. This method uses a relatively inexpensive solid state sensor that compares the CH band with a reference band to calculate the ethanol content. The calculation makes use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beer-Lambert_law" title="Beer-Lambert law">Beer-Lambert law</a>. Alternatively, by measuring the density of the starting material and the density of the product, using a <a href="/wiki/Hydrometer" title="Hydrometer">hydrometer</a>, the change in specific gravity during fermentation indicates the alcohol content. This inexpensive and indirect method has a long history in the beer brewing industry.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Purification">Purification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Purification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distillation">Distillation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Distillation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethylene hydration or brewing produces an ethanol–water mixture. For most industrial and fuel uses, the ethanol must be purified. <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">Fractional distillation</a> at atmospheric pressure can concentrate ethanol to 95.6% by weight (89.5 mole%). This mixture is an <a href="/wiki/Azeotrope" title="Azeotrope">azeotrope</a> with a boiling point of 78.1 °C (172.6 °F), and <i>cannot</i> be further purified by distillation. Addition of an entraining agent, such as <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexane" title="Cyclohexane">cyclohexane</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Heptane" title="Heptane">heptane</a>, allows a new ternary azeotrope comprising the ethanol, water, and the entraining agent to be formed. This lower-boiling ternary azeotrope is removed preferentially, leading to water-free ethanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ullmann_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>At pressures less than atmospheric pressure, the composition of the ethanol-water azeotrope shifts to more ethanol-rich mixtures, and at pressures less than 70 <a href="/wiki/Torr" title="Torr">torr</a> (9.333 kPa), there is no azeotrope, and it is possible to distill absolute ethanol from an ethanol-water mixture. While vacuum distillation of ethanol is not presently economical, pressure-swing distillation is a topic of current research. In this technique, a reduced-pressure distillation first yields an ethanol-water mixture of more than 95.6% ethanol. Then, fractional distillation of this mixture at atmospheric pressure distills off the 95.6% azeotrope, leaving anhydrous ethanol at the bottom.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_sieves_and_desiccants">Molecular sieves and desiccants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Molecular sieves and desiccants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(December 2015)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Apart from distillation, ethanol may be dried by addition of a <a href="/wiki/Desiccant" title="Desiccant">desiccant</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_sieves" title="Molecular sieves">molecular sieves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cornmeal" title="Cornmeal">cornmeal</a>. The desiccants can be dried and reused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ullmann_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Molecular_sieve" title="Molecular sieve">Molecular sieves</a> can be used to selectively absorb the water from the 95.6% ethanol solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Synthetic <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">zeolite</a> in pellet form can be used, as well as a variety of plant-derived absorbents, including <a href="/wiki/Cornmeal" title="Cornmeal">cornmeal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Straw" title="Straw">straw</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sawdust" title="Sawdust">sawdust</a>. The zeolite bed can be regenerated essentially an unlimited number of times by drying it with a blast of hot <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>. Cornmeal and other plant-derived absorbents cannot readily be regenerated, but where ethanol is made from grain, they are often available at low cost. Absolute ethanol produced this way has no residual benzene, and can be used to fortify port and sherry in traditional winery operations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Membranes_and_reverse_osmosis">Membranes and reverse osmosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Membranes and reverse osmosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Membranes can also be used to separate ethanol and water. Membrane-based separations are not subject to the limitations of the water-ethanol azeotrope because the separations are not based on vapor-liquid equilibria. Membranes are often used in the so-called hybrid membrane distillation process. This process uses a pre-concentration distillation column as first separating step. The further separation is then accomplished with a membrane operated either in vapor permeation or pervaporation mode. Vapor permeation uses a vapor membrane feed and pervaporation uses a liquid membrane feed.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_techniques">Other techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Other techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A variety of other techniques have been discussed, including the following:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ullmann_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Salting using <a href="/wiki/Potassium_carbonate" title="Potassium carbonate">potassium carbonate</a> to exploit its insolubility will cause a phase separation with ethanol and water. This offers a very small potassium carbonate impurity to the alcohol that can be removed by distillation. This method is very useful in purification of ethanol by distillation, as ethanol forms an <a href="/wiki/Azeotrope" title="Azeotrope">azeotrope</a> with water.</li>
<li>Direct <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_reduction_of_carbon_dioxide" title="Electrochemical reduction of carbon dioxide">electrochemical reduction of carbon dioxide</a> to ethanol under ambient conditions using <a href="/wiki/Copper_nanoparticle" title="Copper nanoparticle">copper nanoparticles</a> on a carbon nanospike film as the catalyst;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Extraction of ethanol from grain mash by <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_carbon_dioxide" title="Supercritical carbon dioxide">supercritical carbon dioxide</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pervaporation" title="Pervaporation">Pervaporation</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fractional_freezing" title="Fractional freezing">Fractional freezing</a> is also used to concentrate fermented alcoholic solutions, such as traditionally made <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Applejack_(beverage)" title="Applejack (beverage)">Applejack (beverage)</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pressure_swing_adsorption" title="Pressure swing adsorption">Pressure swing adsorption</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grades_of_ethanol">Grades of ethanol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Grades of ethanol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Denatured_alcohol">Denatured alcohol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Denatured alcohol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Denatured_alcohol" title="Denatured alcohol">Denatured alcohol</a></div>
<p>Pure ethanol and alcoholic beverages are heavily <a href="/wiki/Sin_tax" title="Sin tax">taxed</a> as psychoactive drugs, but ethanol has many uses that do not involve its consumption. To relieve the tax burden on these uses, most jurisdictions waive the tax when an agent has been added to the ethanol to render it unfit to drink. These include <a href="/wiki/Bitterant" title="Bitterant">bittering agents</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denatonium_benzoate" title="Denatonium benzoate">denatonium benzoate</a> and toxins such as <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naphtha" title="Naphtha">naphtha</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pyridine" title="Pyridine">pyridine</a>. Products of this kind are called <i>denatured alcohol.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Absolute_alcohol">Absolute alcohol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Absolute alcohol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Absolute or anhydrous alcohol refers to ethanol with a low water content. There are various grades with maximum water contents ranging from 1% to a few parts per million (ppm) levels. If <a href="/wiki/Azeotropic_distillation" title="Azeotropic distillation">azeotropic distillation</a> is used to remove water, it will contain trace amounts of the material separation agent (e.g. benzene).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Absolute alcohol is not intended for human consumption. Absolute ethanol is used as a solvent for laboratory and industrial applications, where water will react with other chemicals, and as fuel alcohol. Spectroscopic ethanol is an absolute ethanol with a low absorbance in <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> and visible light, fit for use as a solvent in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet-visible_spectroscopy" title="Ultraviolet-visible spectroscopy">ultraviolet-visible spectroscopy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pure ethanol is classed as 200 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proof_(alcohol)" title="Proof (alcohol)">proof</a> in the U.S., equivalent to 175 degrees proof in the UK system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrews2007_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrews2007-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rectified_spirits">Rectified spirits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Rectified spirits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Rectified spirit, an azeotropic composition of 96% ethanol containing 4% water, is used instead of anhydrous ethanol for various purposes. Wine spirits are about 94% ethanol (188 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proof_(alcohol)" title="Proof (alcohol)">proof</a>). The impurities are different from those in 95% (190 proof) laboratory ethanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions">Reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">Alcohol</a></div>
<p>Ethanol is classified as a primary alcohol, meaning that the carbon its hydroxyl group attaches to has at least two hydrogen atoms attached to it as well. Many ethanol reactions occur at its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> group.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ester_formation">Ester formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Ester formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the presence of acid catalysts, ethanol reacts with <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acids</a> to produce ethyl <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a> and water:
</p>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">RCOOH</a> + HOCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub> → <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">RCOOCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub></a> + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>This reaction, which is conducted on large scale industrially, requires the removal of the water from the reaction mixture as it is formed. Esters react in the presence of an acid or base to give back the alcohol and a salt. This reaction is known as <a href="/wiki/Saponification" title="Saponification">saponification</a> because it is used in the preparation of soap. Ethanol can also form esters with inorganic acids. <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_sulfate" title="Diethyl sulfate">Diethyl sulfate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triethyl_phosphate" title="Triethyl phosphate">triethyl phosphate</a> are prepared by treating ethanol with sulfur trioxide and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_pentoxide" title="Phosphorus pentoxide">phosphorus pentoxide</a> respectively. <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_sulfate" title="Diethyl sulfate">Diethyl sulfate</a> is a useful ethylating agent in <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_nitrite" title="Ethyl nitrite">Ethyl nitrite</a>, prepared from the reaction of ethanol with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrite" title="Sodium nitrite">sodium nitrite</a> and sulfuric acid, was formerly used as a <a href="/wiki/Diuretic" title="Diuretic">diuretic</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dehydration">Dehydration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Dehydration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Strong acid desiccants cause the partial dehydration of ethanol to form <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">diethyl ether</a> and other byproducts. If the dehydration temperature exceeds around 160 °C (320 °F), full dehydration will occur and <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a> will be the main product.
</p>
<dl><dd>2 CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH → <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub></a> + H<sub>2</sub>O (ca. 120 °C)</dd>
<dd>   CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH → <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">H2C=CH2</a> + H<sub>2</sub>O (above 160 °C)</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combustion">Combustion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Combustion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Complete <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> of ethanol forms <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH (l) + 3 O<sub>2</sub> (g) → 2 CO<sub>2</sub> (g) + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O (l); −ΔH<sub>c</sub> = 1371 kJ/mol<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> = 29.8 kJ/g = 327 kcal/mol = 7.1 kcal/g</dd></dl>
<dl><dd>C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH (l) + 3 O<sub>2</sub> (g) → 2 CO<sub>2</sub> (g) + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O (g); −ΔH<sub>c</sub> = 1236 kJ/mol = 26.8 kJ/g = 295.4 kcal/mol = 6.41 kcal/g<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Specific heat = 2.44 kJ/(kg·K)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acid-base_chemistry">Acid-base chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Acid-base chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethanol is a neutral molecule and the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of a solution of ethanol in water is nearly 7.00. Ethanol can be quantitatively converted to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjugate_base" title="Conjugate base">conjugate base</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Alkoxide" title="Alkoxide">ethoxide</a> ion (CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>O<sup>−</sup>), by reaction with an <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metal</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m_and_b_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-m_and_b-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH + 2 Na → 2 CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>ONa + H<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>or a very strong base such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydride" title="Sodium hydride">sodium hydride</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH + NaH → CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>ONa + H<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>The acidity of water and ethanol are nearly the same, as indicated by their <a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant" title="Acid dissociation constant">pKa</a> of 15.7 and 16 respectively. Thus, sodium ethoxide and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> exist in an equilibrium that is closely balanced:
</p>
<dl><dd>CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH + NaOH ⇌ CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>ONa + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halogenation">Halogenation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Halogenation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethanol is not used industrially as a precursor to ethyl halides, but the reactions are illustrative. Ethanol reacts with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_halide" title="Hydrogen halide">hydrogen halides</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Haloalkane" title="Haloalkane">ethyl halides</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethyl_chloride" title="Ethyl chloride">ethyl chloride</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethyl_bromide" title="Ethyl bromide">ethyl bromide</a> via an <a href="/wiki/SN2_reaction" title="SN2 reaction">S<sub>N</sub>2 reaction</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH + <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">HCl</a> → CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>Cl + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>These reactions require a catalyst such as <a href="/wiki/Zinc_chloride" title="Zinc chloride">zinc chloride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s_and_h_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-s_and_h-78">[78]</a></sup>
HBr requires <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refluxing" title="Refluxing">refluxing</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> catalyst.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s_and_h_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-s_and_h-78">[78]</a></sup> Ethyl halides can, in principle, also be produced by treating ethanol with more specialized <a href="/wiki/Halogenation" title="Halogenation">halogenating agents</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Thionyl_chloride" title="Thionyl chloride">thionyl chloride</a> or <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_tribromide" title="Phosphorus tribromide">phosphorus tribromide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m_and_b_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-m_and_b-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s_and_h_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-s_and_h-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH + SOCl<sub>2</sub> → CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>Cl + SO<sub>2</sub> + HCl</dd></dl>
<p>Upon treatment with halogens in the presence of base, ethanol gives the corresponding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haloform" title="Haloform">haloform</a> (CHX<sub>3</sub>, where X = Cl, Br, I). This conversion is called the <a href="/wiki/Haloform_reaction" title="Haloform reaction">haloform reaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> "
An intermediate in the reaction with chlorine is the <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehyde</a> called <a href="/wiki/Chloral" title="Chloral">chloral</a>, which forms <a href="/wiki/Chloral_hydrate" title="Chloral hydrate">chloral hydrate</a> upon reaction with water:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ull_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ull-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>4 Cl<sub>2</sub> + CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH → CCl<sub>3</sub>CHO + 5 HCl</dd>
<dd>CCl<sub>3</sub>CHO + H<sub>2</sub>O → CCl<sub>3</sub>C(OH)<sub>2</sub>H</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxidation">Oxidation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Oxidation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethanol can be oxidized to <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde" title="Acetaldehyde">acetaldehyde</a> and further oxidized to <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, depending on the reagents and conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s_and_h_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-s_and_h-78">[78]</a></sup> This oxidation is of no importance industrially, but in the human body, these oxidation reactions are catalyzed by the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liver_alcohol_dehydrogenase" title="Liver alcohol dehydrogenase">liver alcohol dehydrogenase</a>. The oxidation product of ethanol, acetic acid, is a nutrient for humans, being a precursor to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetyl_CoA" title="Acetyl CoA">acetyl CoA</a>, where the acetyl group can be spent as energy or used for biosynthesis.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Alcohol#Toxicity" title="Alcohol">Alcohol § Toxicity</a></div>
<p>Pure ethanol will irritate the skin and eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Nausea, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, and intoxication are symptoms of ingestion. Long-term use by ingestion can result in serious liver damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msdset_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-msdset-100">[100]</a></sup> Atmospheric concentrations above one in a thousand are above the European Union <a href="/wiki/Occupational_exposure_limit" title="Occupational exposure limit">occupational exposure limits</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msdset_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-msdset-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distilled_beverage" title="Distilled beverage">Distilled beverage</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermentation</a> of sugar into ethanol is one of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnologies</a> employed by humans. The intoxicating effects of ethanol consumption have been known since ancient times. Ethanol has been used by humans since prehistory as the intoxicating ingredient of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcoholic beverages</a>. Dried residue on 9,000-year-old pottery found in China suggests that <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> people consumed alcoholic beverages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roach_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roach-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Alchemy_and_chemistry_in_medieval_Islam" title="Alchemy and chemistry in medieval Islam">medieval Muslims</a> used the <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a> process extensively, and applied it to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distilled_beverage" title="Distilled beverage">distillation of alcohol</a>. The Arab chemist <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">Al-Kindi</a> unambiguously described the distillation of wine in the 9th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hassan_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-hassan-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hassan-Alcohol_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan-Alcohol-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The process later spread from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> to Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hassan_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-hassan-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habib_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-habib-105">[105]</a></sup> Production of alcohol from distilled wine was later recorded by the <a href="/wiki/Schola_Medica_Salernitana" title="Schola Medica Salernitana">School of Salerno</a> alchemists in the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-106">[106]</a></sup> Mention of absolute alcohol, in contrast with alcohol-water mixtures, was later made by <a href="/wiki/Ramon_Llull" title="Ramon Llull">Raymond Lull</a> in the 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, archaeological evidence indicates that the true distillation of alcohol began during the 12th century <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Song_dynasty" title="Southern Song dynasty">Southern Song</a> dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haw_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-haw-107">[107]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Still" title="Still">still</a> has been found at an archaeological site in Qinglong, <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a>, dating to the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haw_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-haw-107">[107]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, the true distillation of alcohol was introduced from the Middle East, and was in wide use in the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> by the 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habib_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-habib-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1796, German-Russian chemist Johann Tobias Lowitz obtained pure ethanol by mixing partially purified ethanol (the alcohol-water azeotrope) with an excess of anhydrous alkali and then distilling the mixture over low heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> French chemist <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> described ethanol as a compound of carbon, hydrogen, and oxygen, and in 1807 <a href="/wiki/Nicolas-Th%C3%A9odore_de_Saussure" title="Nicolas-Théodore de Saussure">Nicolas-Théodore de Saussure</a> determined ethanol's chemical formula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Fifty years later, <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Scott_Couper" title="Archibald Scott Couper">Archibald Scott Couper</a> published the structural formula of ethanol. It was one of the first structural formulas determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Couper_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Couper-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ethanol was first prepared synthetically in 1825 by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a>. He found that sulfuric acid could absorb large volumes of <a href="/wiki/Coal_gas" title="Coal gas">coal gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> He gave the resulting solution to <a href="/wiki/Henry_Hennell" title="Henry Hennell">Henry Hennell</a>, a British chemist, who found in 1826 that it contained "sulphovinic acid" (<a href="/wiki/Ethyl_sulfate" title="Ethyl sulfate">ethyl hydrogen sulfate</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> In 1828, Hennell and the French chemist <a href="/wiki/Georges-Simon_Serullas" title="Georges-Simon Serullas">Georges-Simon Serullas</a> independently discovered that sulphovinic acid could be decomposed into ethanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hennell_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hennell-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Thus, in 1825 Faraday had unwittingly discovered that ethanol could be produced from <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a> (a component of coal gas) by <a href="/wiki/Acid_catalysis" title="Acid catalysis">acid-catalyzed</a> hydration, a process similar to current industrial ethanol synthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ethanol was used as lamp fuel in the United States as early as 1840, but a tax levied on industrial alcohol during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> made this use uneconomical. The tax was repealed in 1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegel_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-siegel-117">[117]</a></sup> Use as an automotive fuel dates back to 1908, with the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Model_T" title="Ford Model T">Ford Model T</a> able to run on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petrol" title="Petrol">petrol</a> (gasoline) or ethanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dipardo_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-dipardo-118">[118]</a></sup> It fuels some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirit_lamps" title="Spirit lamps">spirit lamps</a>.
</p><p>Ethanol intended for industrial use is often produced from <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-myers_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-myers-119">[119]</a></sup> Ethanol has widespread use as a solvent of substances intended for human contact or consumption, including scents, flavorings, colorings, and medicines. In chemistry, it is both a solvent and a feedstock for the synthesis of other products. It has a long history as a fuel for heat and light, and more recently as a fuel for <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a>.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-112"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-112">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Faraday M (1825). <a class="external text" href="http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/bpt6k559209/f473.image" rel="nofollow">"On new compounds of carbon and hydrogen, and on certain other products obtained during the decomposition of oil by heat"</a>. <i>Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society of London</i>. <b>115</b>: 440–466. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1098%2Frstl.1825.0022" rel="nofollow">10.1098/rstl.1825.0022</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Philosophical+Transactions+of+the+Royal+Society+of+London&amp;rft.atitle=On+new+compounds+of+carbon+and+hydrogen%2C+and+on+certain+other+products+obtained+during+the+decomposition+of+oil+by+heat&amp;rft.volume=115&amp;rft.pages=440-466&amp;rft.date=1825&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1098%2Frstl.1825.0022&amp;rft.aulast=Faraday&amp;rft.aufirst=M&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fgallica.bnf.fr%2Fark%3A%2F12148%2Fbpt6k559209%2Ff473.image&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEthanol"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/> In a footnote on page 448, Faraday notes the action of sulfuric acid on coal gas and coal-gas distillate; specifically, "The [sulfuric] acid combines directly with carbon and hydrogen; and I find when [the resulting compound is] united with bases [it] forms a peculiar class of salts, somewhat resembling the sulphovinates [i.e., ethyl sulfates], but still different from them."</span>

<li id="cite_note-113"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-113">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Hennell H (1826). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=f05FAAAAcAAJ" rel="nofollow">"On the mutual action of sulphuric acid and alcohol, with observations on the composition and properties of the resulting compound"</a>. <i>Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society of London</i>. <b>116</b>: 240–249. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1098%2Frstl.1826.0021" rel="nofollow">10.1098/rstl.1826.0021</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Philosophical+Transactions+of+the+Royal+Society+of+London&amp;rft.atitle=On+the+mutual+action+of+sulphuric+acid+and+alcohol%2C+with+observations+on+the+composition+and+properties+of+the+resulting+compound&amp;rft.volume=116&amp;rft.pages=240-249&amp;rft.date=1826&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1098%2Frstl.1826.0021&amp;rft.aulast=Hennell&amp;rft.aufirst=H&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Df05FAAAAcAAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEthanol"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/> On page 248, Hennell mentions that Faraday gave him some sulfuric acid in which coal gas had dissolved and that he (Hennell) found that it contained "sulphovinic acid" (ethyl hydrogen sulfate).</span>

<li id="cite_note-Hennell-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Hennell_114-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Hennell H (1828). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=X-9FAAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA365" rel="nofollow">"On the mutual action of sulfuric acid and alcohol, and on the nature of the process by which ether is formed"</a>. <i>Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society of London</i>. <b>118</b>: 365–371. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1098%2Frstl.1828.0021" rel="nofollow">10.1098/rstl.1828.0021</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Philosophical+Transactions+of+the+Royal+Society+of+London&amp;rft.atitle=On+the+mutual+action+of+sulfuric+acid+and+alcohol%2C+and+on+the+nature+of+the+process+by+which+ether+is+formed&amp;rft.volume=118&amp;rft.pages=365-371&amp;rft.date=1828&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1098%2Frstl.1828.0021&amp;rft.aulast=Hennell&amp;rft.aufirst=H&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DX-9FAAAAMAAJ%26pg%3DPA365&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEthanol"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/> On page 368, Hennell produces ethanol from "sulfovinic acid" (<a href="/wiki/Ethyl_sulfate" title="Ethyl sulfate">ethyl hydrogen sulfate</a>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-115"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-115">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Sérullas G (1828).  Guyton de Morveau L, Gay-Lussac JL, Arago F, Michel Eugène Chevreul, Marcellin Berthelot, Éleuthère Élie Nicolas Mascart, Albin Haller, eds. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=ZxUAAAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA152" rel="nofollow">"De l'action de l'acide sulfurique sur l'alcool, et des produits qui en résultent"</a>. <i>Annales de Chimie et de Physique</i>. <b>39</b>: 152–186.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Annales+de+Chimie+et+de+Physique&amp;rft.atitle=De+l%27action+de+l%27acide+sulfurique+sur+l%27alcool%2C+et+des+produits+qui+en+r%C3%A9sultent&amp;rft.volume=39&amp;rft.pages=152-186&amp;rft.date=1828&amp;rft.aulast=S%C3%A9rullas&amp;rft.aufirst=Georges-Simon&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DZxUAAAAAMAAJ%26pg%3DPA152&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEthanol"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/> On page 158, Sérullas mentions the production of alcohol from "sulfate acid d'hydrogène carboné" (hydrocarbon acid sulfate).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1855, the French chemist <a href="/wiki/Marcellin_Berthelot" title="Marcellin Berthelot">Marcellin Berthelot</a> confirmed Faraday's discovery by preparing ethanol from pure ethylene. <cite class="citation journal">Berthelot M (1855).  Arago F, Gay-Lussac JL, eds. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=1ClCAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA385" rel="nofollow">"Sur la formation de l'alcool au moyen du bicarbure d'hydrogène (On the formation of alcohol by means of ethylene)"</a>. <i>Annales de Chimie et de Physique</i>. <b>43</b>: 385–405.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Annales+de+Chimie+et+de+Physique&amp;rft.atitle=Sur+la+formation+de+l%27alcool+au+moyen+du+bicarbure+d%27hydrog%C3%A8ne+%28On+the+formation+of+alcohol+by+means+of+ethylene%29&amp;rft.volume=43&amp;rft.pages=385-405&amp;rft.date=1855&amp;rft.aulast=Berthelot&amp;rft.aufirst=Marcellin&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D1ClCAAAAcAAJ%26pg%3DPA385&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEthanol"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/> (Note: The chemical formulas in Berthelot's paper are wrong because chemists at that time used the wrong atomic masses for the elements; e.g., carbon (6 instead of 12), oxygen (8 instead of 16), etc.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-siegel-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-siegel_117-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Siegel R (15 February 2007). <a class="external text" href="https://www.npr.org/templates/story/story.php?storyId=7426827" rel="nofollow">"Ethanol, Once Bypassed, Now Surging Ahead"</a>. </li>
<li id="cite_note-dipardo-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-dipardo_118-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">DiPardo J. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150924050511/http://www.eia.gov/oiaf/analysispaper/pdf/biomass.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Outlook for Biomass Ethanol Production and Demand"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>.
